Jamison Brereton

8 (834)
Agni (1‚Äì6), Indra (7‚Äì9)
Tri≈õiras TvƒÅ·π£·π≠ra
9 verses: tri·π£·π≠ubh
Although the Anukrama·πáƒ´ assigns this hymn, along with the following one (X.9 to the Waters), to a different poet from the first seven Agni hymns of the ma·πá·∏çala, which are attributed to Trita ƒÄptya, it is fairly clear that these nine hymns belong together, on the basis of criteria like shared phraseology, as well as larger structural considerations. (See Oldenberg 1888: 233‚Äì34.) The poet of X.8‚Äì9 is given as Tri≈õiras TvƒÅ·π£·π≠ra, a speaking name: ‚ÄúThree-Headed Son of Tva·π£·π≠ar‚Äù and a figure who is the mythological enemy of Trita. Thus, as Oldenberg saw, the two poets to whom this sequence of nine hymns is ascribed are narratively linked, and both names were probably assigned as poets to these hymns because of the mythological narrative sketched in this particular hymn. Trita‚Äôs defeat of his three-headed foe, also known as Vi≈õvar≈´pa (‚Äúhaving all forms‚Äù), is treated in the last three verses of our hymn (7‚Äì9). The myth goes back to Indo-Iranian times in one form or other. In the Younger Avestan Hom Ya≈°t (Yasna 9‚Äì11), Zarathushtra asks the Haoma (= Skt. Soma) the names and stories of the men who first pressed him. In Y 9.7‚Äì8 Haoma names a certain ƒÄœëŒ≤iia (note the similarity to, but not identity with, ƒÄpt(i)ya) as the second man to press him, whose son, Œòraƒìtaona, smote a mighty serpent-dragon (A≈æi DahƒÅka; cf. the Sanskrit cognate √°hi ‚Äúserpent‚Äù frequently used of VrÃ•tra) with three heads (œërikam«ùr«ùŒ¥a; cf. m≈´rdh√°n ‚Äúhead‚Äù in vss. 3 and 6 of this hymn). Immediately thereafter (Y 9.10‚Äì11) he identifies Œòrita (= Skt. Trita) as his third presser, one of whose sons defeats another formidable dragon (also a≈æi) in a particularly imaginative fashion. The names ƒÄœëŒ≤iia and Œòrita, though differently distributed, and the three-headed mon ster speak strongly for the kinship of the two myths, though they are not identical in all their features.
What is not clear is why this little mythic vignette has been appended at the end of this Agni cycle, though we will suggest some reasons at the end of the introduc tion. Here and elsewhere in Vedic the story of Trita ƒÄptya‚Äôs slaying of three-headed Vi≈õvar≈´pa is associated with Indra (even in this hymn; see vss. 8‚Äì9), not Agni, and it is assimilated in several ways to the better-known Vala myth‚Äîgenerally an Indra myth. First, Trita ƒÄptya begins his quest in a cave or hole of some sort (vs. 7a), and the happy result of the defeat of Vi≈õvar≈´pa is the release of his cows (vss. 8‚Äì9)‚Äîjust as the cows confined in the Vala cave are released at the end of that myth. Moreover, the ‚Äúfamilial‚Äù (vs. 7) or ‚Äúancestral‚Äù (vs. 8) weapons Trita employs in the fight appear to be mental and verbal (see especially the ‚Äúvisionary thought‚Äù he seeks in 7b and his ‚Äúspeaking‚Äù his weapons in 7d). In the Vala myth Indra and his helpers, the A·πÖgirases, break open the cave with verbal spells, not physical weapons. Since the corresponding Avestan version does not have these elements, it is likely that these Vala-like features were grafted onto the inherited myth, whose popularity was per
haps waning in RÃ•gvedic times. (It does have a robust revival in Vedic prose.) The first few verses of the hymn for the most part contain standard Agni fare, though with a cosmic dimension. The fire, conceived of also as a powerful male animal, reaches through the worlds to heaven itself (vs. 1), just after it is born (vs. 2). His birth and installation are further treated in verse 3, where the ritual references probably also have cosmic referents. The hymn begins to take an odd turn in verse 4. In that verse Agni ‚Äúsets seven steps,‚Äù thereby ‚Äúbegetting an alliance (or, the god Mitra).‚Äù The seven-step reference is to the culminating ritual action particularly of the marriage ceremony, after which the marriage is irrevocable, when the bride and groom take seven steps together; other alliances can be assimilated to the mar riage alliance by the performance of the seven steps (see Jamison 1996a: 120‚Äì23). These seven steps are always taken beside the ritual fire, and as Proferes (2007) has discussed at length (on this particular passage, see p. 61), the fire is also a symbol of the alliances made by social groups. Through those alliances the fire itself is strengthened and increased, hence the ‚Äúfor the sake of your own body‚Äù in this verse. The reason for this intrusion of the social world at this point in the hymn is unclear,

since heretofore the focus has been entirely on Agni. In the following verses (5‚Äì6) Agni is identified with, or transformed into, a series of divinities and functional roles. Perhaps these identifications were suggested by the alliance generated in verse 4, representing the web of associations in which Agni participates.
The six Agni verses end with Agni setting his head (m≈´rdh√°n) in heaven. As noted above, the hymn in fact contains two occurrences of m≈´rdh√°n (vss. 3, 6), and its Avestan cognate kam«ùr«ùŒ¥an (with pejorative ka-prefix), in the bahuvrƒ´hi Œòri-kam«ùr«ùŒ¥a ‚Äúhaving three heads,‚Äù qualifies the monster killed by ƒÄœëŒ≤iia‚Äôs son. The corresponding form in the Trita myth in this hymn has a different word for head, -≈õ√≠ras- (acc. sg. m. tri-≈õƒ´r·π£ƒÅÃÅ·πáam, vs. 8). However, the RÃ•gveda does contain an occurrence of tri-m≈´rdh√°n‚Äîbut referring to Agni, in the phrase trim≈´rdhƒÅÃÅna·πÉ
sapt√°ra≈õmim ‚Äúhaving three heads and seven reins‚Äù (I.146.1). The three heads are most likely the three ritual fires, the seven reins possibly the seven priests, but the important thing is that this same phrase, ‚Äúhaving three heads and seven reins,‚Äù is found in our hymn X.8, describing the enemy Vi≈õvar≈´pa (8c). This, we would submit, is the initial point of contact between this Agni hymn and the Trita saga appended to it: the old Indo-Iranian myth used the word *tri-mrÃ•Hdhan for the three-headed beast. But the epithet can also be used positively as a descriptor of Agni with his three representative fires on the ritual ground. The poet is playing with the delicious positive/negative polarities of the epithet, and signals the connection between the Agni portion of the hymn and the little Trita myth by using the word originally used of the monster, m≈´rdh√°n, elsewhere in the hymn.
But there may be another connection. As we noted, Agni undergoes several transformations in the last verses of his section of the hymn. And it is quite possible that verse 7 is suggesting yet another transformation, into Trita or ‚Äúthe third (fire),‚Äù being born in the fireplace, seeking poetic vision and ritual release. Agni sometimes seems to be called Trita (see esp. X.46.3, 6), and the Trita/Vi≈õvar≈´pa story may be introduced here via a modulation through Agni identified with Trita.

‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§É ‡§¨‡•É‡§π‡§§‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§π‡§§‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á‡§§‡•Å‡§®‡§æ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§® ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§É ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç ‡§Ü ‡§á‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§∞‡•ã‡§¶‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡•å ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡§ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡•á‡§£ ‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§§‡§ø ‡•§ ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ç‡§ö ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§µ‡•É‡§∑‡§≠‡§É ‡§á‡§µ ‡§∞‡•ã‡§∞‡§µ‡•Ä‡§§‡§ø ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ç ‡§∂‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ã‡§§‡§ø ‡•§ ‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡§ø ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§∏‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§â‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§™‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§ú‡•ç‡§µ‡§≤‡§®‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡§æ ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§§‡§É ‡§â‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§ü‡•ç ‡§ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§®‡•ã‡§§‡§ø ‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§™‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø‡§≤‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§£‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§¶‡§ï‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§â‡§™‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡•á ‡§â‡§™‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§Ω‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á ‡§µ‡•à‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§π‡§ø‡§∑‡§É ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§µ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§§‡•á ‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúAgni traverses heaven and earth with a vast banner; he roars (like) a bull; he spreads aloft over theremote and proximate (regions) of the sky; mighty, he increases in the lap of the water.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

Agni traverses: as thelightning in the firmament

Jamison Brereton

With his lofty beacon Agni drives forth; the bull keeps bellowing to the two world-halves.
He has reached up even to the farthest ends of heaven; the buffalo has grown strong in the lap of the waters.

Griffith

AGNI advances with his lofty banner: the Bull is bellowing to the earth and heavens.
He hath attained the sky’s supremest limits. the Steer hath waxen in the lap of waters.

Geldner

Mit hohem Banner geht Agni voran; der Bulle br√ºllt beide Welten an. Er reicht hinauf selbst bis zu den √§u√üersten Enden des Himmels. Im Scho√üe der Gew√§sser ist der B√ºffel gro√ü geworden.

Grassmann

Es schreitet Agni vor mit hohem Banner, und Erd’ und Himmel br√ºllet laut der Stier an; Auch zu des Himmels h√∂chsten Enden drang er, im Schooss der Wasser wurde gross der B√ºffel.

‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§É) ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§®‡•ç-‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§É (‡§¨‡•É‡§π‡§§‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á‡§§‡•Å‡§®‡§æ) ‡§Æ‡§π‡§§‡§æ ‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§™‡§ï‡•á‡§® ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡•á‡§® (‡§∞‡•ã‡§¶‡§∏‡•Ä) ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡•å-‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ø‡§µ‡•Ä‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§ú‡§ó‡§§‡•ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡§ø) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§§‡§ø-‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§®‡•ã‡§§‡§ø, ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó ‡§â‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á (‡§µ‡•É‡§∑‡§≠‡§É-‡§∞‡•ã‡§∞‡§µ‡•Ä‡§§‡§ø) ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡•á‡§£ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡•ç‡§µ‡§≤‡§®‡•ç ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç ‡§µ‡•É‡§∑‡§≠ ‡§á‡§µ ‡§≠‡•É‡§∂‡§Ç ‡§∂‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§Æ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§É (‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡§É-‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ç-‡§â‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç-‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡•á‡§£ ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡§æ‡§®‡•Å‡§™‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡§ø‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§ñ‡§≤‡•ç‡§µ‡§™‡§ø (‡§â‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§ü‡•ç) ‡§â‡§™‡§∞‡§ø ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§®‡•ã‡§§‡§ø (‡§Ö‡§™‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç-‡§â‡§™‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡•á) ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‚Äú‡§Ü‡§™‡•ã‡§Ω‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‚Äù [‡§®‡§ø‡§ò‡•¶ ‡•ß‡•§‡•©]‡§Æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á ‡§Ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ-‡§Ö‡§™‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ú‡§≤‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•á-‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡•á‡§£ (‡§Æ‡§π‡§ø‡§∑‡§É-‡§µ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß) ‡§è‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç ‡§µ‡•É‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§Ω‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡••‡•ß‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúTHe embryo (of heaven and earth), the showerer (of benefits), the glorious, rejoices; the excellentchild (of morn and eve), the celebrator of holy rites calls aloud; assiduous in exertions at the worship of the gods,he moves chief in his own abodes.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

He rejoices as an embryo, as a hump-backed bull. No miscarriage, the calf has bellowed vehemently.
Causing (the oblations) to be lifted up to the conclave of gods, he goes first within his own dwelling places.

Griffith

The Bull, the youngling with the hump, hath frolicked, the strong and never-ceasing Calf hath bellowed.
Bringing our offerings to the God’s assembly, he moves as Chief in his own dwelling-places.

Geldner

Das neugeborene Kind ist fr√∂hlich, der Bulle mit dem Buckel; das Kalb, das keine Fehlgeburt ist, hat heftig gebr√ºllt. Bei dem G√∂tteropfer die dargebrachten Spenden ausrichtend, zieht er als Erster in seine Wohnst√§tten ein.

Grassmann

Es ist voll Lust der Buckelstier, der junge, es br√ºllt das Kalb sich unerm√ºdlich regend, Zur G√∂tterschar das Dargereichte bringend kommt als der erste er zu seinen Sitzen.

‡§Ø‡§É ‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§É ‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§É ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡§æ‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡•É‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§Ø‡•ã‡§∞‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§É ‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§Ü ‡§Ö‡§∞‡§¨‡•ç‡§ß ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•á‡§ú‡•ã‡§≠‡§ø‡§∞‡§æ‡§∞‡§≠‡§§‡•á ‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡•§ ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡§æ‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡•É‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§Ø‡•ã‡§∞‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡•ã‡§µ‡§§‡•ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ß‡§æ‡§®‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§•‡§®‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§Ü‡§ô‡•Ä‡§∑‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•á‡•§ ‡§Ü‡§∞‡§¨‡•ç‡§ß ‡§à‡§∑‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡•§ ‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§•‡§®‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤‡•á‡§Ω‡§™‡§ø ‡§¶‡§æ‡§π‡•á‡§® ‡§¶‡§ó‡•ç‡§ß‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É ‡•§ ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡§É ‡§∏‡•Å‡§µ‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡•á‡§É ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§É ‡§ó‡§Æ‡§®‡§∂‡•Ä‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§§‡•á‡§ú‡§É ‡§Ö‡§ß‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•á ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡•á ‡§®‡§ø ‡§¶‡§ß‡§ø‡§∞‡•á ‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡§∞‡•ã ‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•á‡§® ‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡•á‡§É ‡§™‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡§§‡§®‡•á ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡•Ä‡§É ‡§Ü‡§∞‡•ã‡§ö‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§É ‡§ã‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§®‡•å ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•á ‡§Ö‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§¨‡•Å‡§ß‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§É ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§≤‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§∂‡§®‡§¨‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡§®‡§æ ‡§µ‡§æ ‡•§ ‡§π‡§µ‡§ø‡§∞‡§æ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É ‡•§ ‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§É ‡§§‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡•Ç‡§É ‡§ú‡•Å‡§∑‡§®‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡§µ‡§Ø‡§É ‡§∏‡•á‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•á ‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúThey have plural ced in the sacrifice the radiance of the powerful Agni, who seizes hold of the forehead ofhis parents, gratifying his cherished, radiant, and expanding limbs, in their course, in their chamber of sacrifice.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

His parents: the parents are either heaven or earth, or the two pieces of touchwood; gratifying…of sacrifice:a≈õvabudhnƒÅ·∏• = vyƒÅptam≈´lƒÅH, with outspread bases, i.e., broad at the bottom and tapering to the top, theusual shape of a fire; in his fight the dawns, drawn by horses, rejoice their bodies in the source of truth (i.e., thesun)

Jamison Brereton

He who has seized the head of his two parents [=Heaven and Earth? kindling sticks?], (him) they [=priests] have set down at the rite as the sun‚Äôs undulating flood.
At his flight the ruddy females [=flames? dawns?] with the horse [=fire?] as their foundation find pleasure in their own bodies within the womb of truth.

Griffith

Him who hath grasped his Parents’ head, they stablished at sacrifice a wave of heavenly lustre.
In his swift flight the red Dawns borne by horses refresh their bodies in the home of Order.

Geldner

Der den Scheitel der beiden Eltern erfa√üt hat, den haben sie im Opfer eingesetzt, ein Meer von Sonne. Bei seinem Aufflug haben die r√∂tlichen Flammen, deren Abschlu√ü die Rosse bilden, an der Wiege der Opferordnung an sich selbst Wohlgefallen gefunden.

Grassmann

Ihn, der der Aeltern Haupt erfasste, setzten sie hin beim Opfer als des Himmels Lichtstrom; Bei seinem Flug im Schooss des Rechts erfreun sich die Morgenr√∂then, die mit Rossen fahren.

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúOpulent Agni, you precede dawn after dawn. You are the illuminator of the twin (day and night);engendering Mitra from your own person n, you retain seven plural ces for sacrifice.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

Mitra: the sun; seven plural ces: theseven altars for the fire: dhis·πáyƒÅ etc.

Jamison Brereton

Because you go at the forefront of every dawn, o good one, you have become the far-radiant one at (the time of) the twins [=Night and
Dawn?].
For truth you have set your seven steps, begetting an alliance [/Mitra] for the sake of your own body.

Griffith

For, Vasu thou precedest every Morning, and still hast been the Twins’ illuminator.
For sacrifice, seven places thou retainest while for thine own self thou engenderest Mitra.

Geldner

Denn du gehst jeder Usas voraus, du Guter; du wardst der Erheller der beiden Zwillinge. Dem Gesetz hast du die sieben Schritte getan, dir selbst in ihm einen Freund schaffend.

Grassmann

Denn du, o guter, f√ºhrst die Morgenr√∂then, du hast erhellt die Schwestern, Nacht und Morgen, Du hast gesetzt des Opfers sieben St√§tten, den Freund erzeugend deinem eignen Leibe.

Elizarenkova

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™ ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Ö‡§™‡§®‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂ ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§§‡•á‡§ú ‡§∏‡•á ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§∏‡•Å‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§Ü‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡§®‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ø‡§µ‡•Ä ‡§™‡§∞ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§ü ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§µ‡§π ‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§∏‡§æ‡§§ ‡§∞‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•Ä ‡§ï‡§ø‡§∞‡§£‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ø‡§µ‡•Ä ‡§™‡§∞ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§ü ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡•Ä ‡§π‡•à, ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§ ‡§Æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§ï‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó ‡§∏‡•á ‡§ï‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Æ ‡§¢‡§Ç‡§ó ‡§∏‡•á ‡§≠‡•Ä ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§ü ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡•Ä ‡§π‡•à‡•§ ‡§á‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ ‡§≠‡•Ä ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡§É‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤ ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ú ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§∞‡§æ‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä-‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§¨‡•ã‡§ß ‡§¶‡•á-‡§∏‡§æ‡§µ‡§ß‡§æ‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡•§ ‡§â‡§®‡•ç‡§π‡•á‡§Ç ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á ‡§ï‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡§∏‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§õ‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§® ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§∏‡§æ‡§§ ‡§Æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§ì‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§¨‡§®‡§æ‡§Ø‡•á ‡§∞‡§ñ‡§®‡•á ‡§ï‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∞‡§ø‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á ‡••‡•™‡••

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§µ‡§∏‡•ã ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§π‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡•á‡§® ‡§§‡•á‡§ú‡§∏‡§æ ‡§ö ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡§É-‡§Ü‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§æ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡§É-‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡•á ! ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™ !‚Äú‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•à ‡§µ‡§∏‡•Å‡§É‚Äù [‡§Æ‡•à‡•¶ ‡•©‡•§‡•™‡•§‡•®] ‡§∏ ‡§è‡§∑‡•ã‡§Ω‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§µ‡§∏‡•Å‡§É [‡§∂‡•¶ ‡•Ø‡•§‡•©‡•§‡•®‡•§‡•ß]‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§â‡§∑‡§É ‡§â‡§∑‡§É-‡§π‡§ø) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∑‡•ã‡§µ‡•á‡§≤‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç-‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§ø-‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç-‡§è‡§∑‡§ø) ‡§ú‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ñ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§®‡•ã‡§∑‡§ø-‡§â‡§¶‡•á‡§∑‡§ø (‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§É-‡§µ‡§ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ-‡§Ö‡§≠‡§µ‡§É) ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ó‡§≤‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§Ø‡•ã‡§∞‡§π‡•ã‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•ã‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§ü‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§≠‡§µ‡§∏‡§ø (‡§ã‡§§‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø‡•à ‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡•á ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§ú‡§®‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç) ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É-‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§É‚Äú‡§∑‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•á ‡§ö‡§§‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§™‡§ø ‡§µ‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç‚Äù‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§ú‡§®‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç-‡§ú‡§®‡§®‡§π‡•á‡§§‡•ã‡§É‚Äú‡§è‡§∑‡§É-‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§É-‡§≠‡§µ‡§§‡§ø ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§É‚Äù [‡§∂‡•¶ ‡•®‡•§‡•©‡•§‡•®‡•§‡•®](‡§∏‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§¶‡§ß‡§ø‡§∑‡•á) ‡§∏‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§∞‡§∂‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§®‡•ç ‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø‡§∏‡§ø ‡••‡•™‡••

‡§Æ‡§π‡§É ‡§Æ‡§π‡§§‡§É ‡§ã‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§π‡•á ‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡•á ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Å‡§É ‡§á‡§µ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§ï‡§É ‡§≠‡•Å‡§µ‡§É ‡§≠‡§µ‡§∏‡§ø ‡•§ ‡§ó‡•ã‡§™‡§æ‡§É ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ï‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§≠‡§µ‡§∏‡§ø ‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ø‡§¶‡§æ ‡§ã‡§§‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§∏‡•ã‡§Æ‡§∞‡§∏‡§≤‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§£‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§¶‡§ï‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§É ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É ‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡§ø ‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§∏‡§ø ‡§§‡§¶‡§æ ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§≠‡•Å‡§µ‡§É ‡§≠‡§µ‡§∏‡§ø ‡•§ ‡§π‡•á ‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡§É ‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡•á ‡§Ö‡§™‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø‡§≤‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§£‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§¶‡§ï‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§®‡§™‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§™‡•å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§≠‡•Å‡§µ‡§É ‡§≠‡§µ‡§∏‡§ø ‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§¶‡•ç‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§π‡§ø ‡§Æ‡•á‡§ò‡•ã ‡§ú‡§æ‡§Ø‡§§‡•á ‡§Æ‡•á‡§ò‡§æ‡§¶‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡•ã ‡§ú‡§æ‡§Ø‡§§ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§™‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á ‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Ø‡§ú‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§π‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§π‡§µ‡§ø‡§É ‡§ú‡•Å‡§ú‡•ã‡§∑‡§É ‡§∏‡•á‡§µ‡§∏‡•á ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§¶‡•Ç‡§§‡§É ‡§≠‡•Å‡§µ‡§É ‡§≠‡§µ‡§∏‡§ø ‡•• ‡••‡•©‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúYou are the eye, the protector of the great sacrifice; when you proceed to the rite, you are Varu·πáa;you are the grandson of the waters, JƒÅtavedas; you are the messenger (of him) whose oblation you enjoy.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

You become the eye and herdsman of great truth; you become Varu·πáa when you pursue (your tasks) for truth.
You become the Child of the Waters, o JƒÅtavedas; you become the
messenger of him in whose oblation you will take pleasure.

Griffith

Thou art the Eye and Guard of mighty Order, and Varuna when to sacrifice thou comest.
Thou art the Waters’ Child O Jatavedas, envoy of him whose offering thou acceptest.

Geldner

Du wardst das Auge und der H√ºter des gro√üen Gesetzes; du wardst Varuna, da du f√ºr das Gesetz eintrittst. Du wardst der Wasser Enkel, o Jatavedas; du wardst der Bote f√ºr den, an dessen Opfer du Gefallen findest.

Grassmann

Du bist des grossen Opfers Hirt und Auge, bist Varuna, wenn du ans heil’ge Werk gehst, Du, Wesenkenner, bist der Spross der Wasser, bist Bote dem, dess Opfer du geniessest.

Elizarenkova

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúYou are the leader of the sacrifice and sacrificial water to the plural ce in which you are associated withthe auspicious steeds of the wind; you sustain the all-enjoying (sun) as chief in heaven; you, Agni, make yourtongue the bearer of the oblation.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

The plural ce: i.e., the firmament; you sustain in heaven: you raise your glorioushead in heaven; you make…oblation: yadƒÅ; when, Agni, you have so done, you are the leade·πõ..; you are theleader of the sacrifice and of water (rain) in the firmament and in heaven (Yajus. 13.15)

Jamison Brereton

You become the leader of the sacrifice and of airy space, where you are accompanied your kindly teams of horses.
In heaven you have set your head, which wins the sun, and you have
made your tongue to be the conveyor of oblations, o Agni.

Griffith

Thou art the Leader of the rite and region, to which with thine auspicious teams thou teadest,
Thy light-bestowing head to heaven thou liftest, making thy tongue the oblationbearer, Agni.

Geldner

Du wardst der F√ºhrer des Opfers und durch das Dunkel, wo du mit deinen freundlichen Gespannen das Geleit gibst. An den Himmel hast du dein das Sonnenlicht gewinnendes Haupt gesetzt; deine Zunge, Agni, hast du zum Opferbef√∂rderer gemacht.

Grassmann

Du bist des Opfers und der L√ºfte Lenker, wohin du eilst mit holden Rossescharen, Du hebst dein Haupt zum Himmel, Glanz erlangend, die Zunge machst du, Agni, Opfer f√ºhrend.

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§ú‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≠‡§æ‡§ú‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ ‡§π‡•à‡•§ ‡§µ‡§π ‡§π‡•ã‡§Æ‡•á ‡§π‡•Å‡§è ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§µ‡§π‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡§ï ‡§≤‡•á ‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡•Ä ‡§π‡•à‡•§ ‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡§§ ‡§Ü‡§π‡•Å‡§§‡§ø‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§ï‡§∞ ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û ‡§∏‡•á ‡§∞‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø ‡§π‡•Å‡§è ‡§¶‡•á‡§∂ ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§∞‡§£ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ ‡§¨‡§®‡§æ‡§§‡•Ä ‡§π‡•à‡•§ ‡§ê‡§∏‡•á ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§æ‡§£‡•Ä ‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§†‡•Ä ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§£‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•Ä ‡§π‡•ã‡§ï‡§∞ ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ú ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§ä‡§™‡§∞ ‡§§‡§ï ‡§™‡§π‡•Å‡§Å‡§ö‡§ï‡§∞ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§∞‡§£ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§Ö‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§æ ‡§¨‡§®‡§æ‡§§‡•Ä ‡§π‡•à ‡••‡•¨‡••

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡•á) ‡§π‡•á ‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡•á ! ‡§Ø‡§¶‡§æ ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç (‡§ú‡§ø‡§π‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§π‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§π‡§Ç ‡§ö‡§ï‡•É‡§∑‡•á) ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§ú‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≠‡§æ‡§ú‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ‡§Ç‚Äú‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≠‡§ú‡§§‡§ø ‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§∏‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ, ‡§§‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‚Äù [‡§Ø‡§ú‡•Å‡•¶ ‡•ß‡•©‡•§‡•ß‡•´ ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§É ] ‚Äú‡§∑‡§£ ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≠‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•å‚Äù [‡§≠‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§É]‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡•ã‡§¢‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ã‡§∑‡§ø (‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡§ø ‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§¶‡§ß‡§ø‡§∑‡•á) ‡§§‡§¶‡§æ ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï‡•á ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç‚Äú‡§è‡§∑ ‡§µ‡•à ‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ ‡§Ø ‡§è‡§∑ ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É ‡§§‡§™‡§§‡§ø [‡§∂‡•¶ ‡•ß‡•©‡•§‡•™‡•§‡•ß‡•§‡•ß‡•©]‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø ‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø‡§∏‡§ø-‡§Ü‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡§∏‡§ø-‡§§‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡•ç, ‡§â‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ‚àí‚Äú‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡•å ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§π‡•Å‡§§‡§ø‡§É ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ó‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§™‡§§‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§§‡§ø‚Äù [‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡•¶ ‡•©‡•§‡•¨‡•≠](‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§∂‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§É-‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§≠‡§ø‡§É-‡§∏‡§ö‡§∏‡•á) ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡§ï‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§£‡§ï‡§∞‡•Ä‡§≠‡§ø‡§É-‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡§§‡§æ‡§π‡•Å‡§§‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§É ‚Äú‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡•ã ‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§®‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‚Äù [‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•Å‡•¶ ‡•´‡•§‡•®‡•≠]‡§∏‡§π ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§µ‡•à‡§§‡§ø (‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∞‡§ú‡§∏‡§É-‡§ö ‡§®‡•á‡§§‡§æ ‡§≠‡•Å‡§µ‡§É) ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§´‡§≤‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∞‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ö ‡§®‡•á‡§§‡§æ ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡§ï‡•ã ‡§≠‡§µ‡§∏‡§ø ‡••‡•¨‡••

‡§™‡§æ‡§£‡§ø‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ‡§≤‡§®‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§ú‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§¶‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§®‡•á‡§® ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡•á‡§® Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúTrita by (his own), desiring a share (of the sacrifice), for the sake of taking part in the exploit of thesupreme protector (of the world), chose (Indra as his friend); attended (by the priests) in the proximity of theparental heaven and earth, and reciting appropriate praise, he takes up his weapons.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

Legend: Indra said tocondition of having a share in the sacrifices offered to Indra. Indra gave him water to wash his hands with and ashare in the sacrifice, whereby Trita’s strength increased; seven-rayed: i.e., seven-tongued, seven- rayed, like thesun, or seven-handed

Jamison Brereton

With his resolve, Trita [/the ‚Äúthird‚Äù fire], within a cave [/cover]‚Äîseeking a visionary thought according to the ways of his distant forefather,
longing for companionship in the lap of his parents, speaking his own familial weapons‚Äîpursues (his tasks).

Griffith

Through his wise insight Trita in the cavern, seeking as ever the Chief Sire’s intention,
Carefully tended in his Parents’ bosom, calling the weapons kin, goes forth to combat.

Geldner

Auf seinen Rat w√ºnschte Trita in der Grube mit Eifer des h√∂chsten Vaters Erkenntnis, und im Scho√üe beider Eltern Beistand suchend verlangt er nach den Waffen, indem er sie als die Verwandtschaftlichen anspricht.

Grassmann

Durch seine Kraft kommt Trita, der im Brunnen Gebet erstrebt nach Art des h√∂chsten Vaters, Nach Huld verlangend zu dem Sitz der Aeltern, verbr√ºdert nennend seine scharfen Waffen.

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ü‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™ ‡§â‡§™‡§æ‡§∏‡§®‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§π‡•á‡§§‡•Å ‡§§‡•Ä‡§® ‡§∂‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§Ü‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§æ‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡§æ ‡§ö‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á‡•§ ‡§¨‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡§® ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§ï‡§æ‡§ü‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§Ö‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¨‡§≤‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™ ‡§∂‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç‡•§ ‡§ú‡•à‡§∏‡•á ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡§æ-‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ü‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§¨‡§æ‡§≤‡§ï ‡§∏‡•Å‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§§ ‡§∞‡§π‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§ê‡§∏‡•á ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§Ü‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ü‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§¨‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡§®‡§∞‡§π‡§ø‡§§ ‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§≠‡§Ø ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•Å‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã ‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡••‡•≠‡••

‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§É) ‡§è‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§§‡•ç‡§ï‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§æ‡§≤‡§ï‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡•É‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡§É (‡§ß‡•Ä‡§§‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ç-‡§á‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§®‡•ç-‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§É) ‡§Ü‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡§£‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§Ü‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§™‡§æ‡§∏‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‚Äú‡§ß‡•Ä‡§ô‡•ç-‡§Ü‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡•á‚Äù [‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§É]‡§§‡§§‡§É‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§®‡•ç ‚Äú‡§ß‡•Ä‡§§‡§ø‡§É-‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡§£‡§æ‚Äù [‡§ã‡•¶ ‡•ß‡•§‡•ß‡•ß‡•Ø‡•§‡•® ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§É]‡§á‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§®‡•ç-‡§µ‡§æ‡§û‡•ç‡§õ‡§®‡•ç ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§É-‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡§É-‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡•Å ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡•Ç‡§≤‡§∏‡•Ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§£‡§∂‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∞‡•á‡§∑‡•Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§É ‡§Ü‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ (‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•Å‡§®‡§æ-‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§É-‡§µ‡§µ‡•ç‡§∞‡•á) ‡§Ö‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§æ ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§æ‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡•á‡§® ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡•á ‡§µ‡•É‡§£‡•ã‡§§‡§ø, ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ (‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§É-‡§â‡§™‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡•á) ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡§æ‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§∞‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•á (‡§∏‡§ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§É) ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§µ‡•à‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç-‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§É (‡§ú‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§£‡§É) ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§á‡§µ ‡§ú‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§•‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡§É (‡§Ü‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ß‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§µ‡•á‡§§‡§ø) ‡§Ö‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¨‡§≤‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§∂‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§®‡•ã‡§§‡§ø ‡••‡•≠‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúHe, the son of the waters, incited by Indra, skilled in his paternal weapons, fought against (the enemy),and slew the seven-rayed, three-headed (asura); then Trita set free the cows of the son of Tva·π£·π≠ƒÅ.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

That one, ƒÄptya, knowing his ancestral weapons, urged on by Indra, attacked.
Having smashed the three-headed, seven-reined (monster), Trita let loose the cows, even those of Tva·π£·π≠ar‚Äôs son.

Griffith

Well-skilled to use the weapons of his Father, Aptya, urged on by Indra, fought the battle.
Then Trita slew the foe seven-rayed, three-headed, and freed the cattle of the Son of Tvastar.
